يضجعه على شقه الأيسر ويغسله لأن التثليث مسنون في غسل الحي فكذا في غسل الميت، أنه ذكرها بقوله وصب عليه ماء مغلي فغير صحيح لأنها ليست غسلة . الثلاث بدليل قوله بعد وغسل رأسه ولحيته بالخطمي فإن السنة أن يبدأ بغسلهما قبل الغسلة الأولى وإنما هو كلام إجمالي لبيان كيفية الماء، والحاصل أن السنة أنه إذا فرغ من وضوئه غسل رأسه ولحيته بالخطمي من غير تسريح ثم يضجعه على شقه الأيسر ويغسله وهذه مرة، ثم على الأيمن كذلك وهذه ثانية ثم يقعده ويمسح بطنه كما ذكر ثم يضجعه على الأيسر فيصب الماء عليه وهذه ثالثة، لكن ذكر خواهر زاده أن المرة الأولى بالماء القراح، والثانية بالماء المغلي فيه سدر أو حرض والثالثة بالماء الذي فيه الكافور. ولم يفصل صاحب الهداية في مياه الغسلات بين القراح وغيره وهو ظاهر كلام الحاكم. وفي فتح القدير : والأولى أن يغسل الأوليان بالسدر ولم يذكر المصنف كمية الصبات. وفي المجتبى يصب الماء عليه عند كل إضباع ثلاث مرات وإن زاد على الثلاث جاز قوله : (ولم يعد غسله) لأن الغسل عرفناه بالنص وقد حصل مرة، وكذا لا تجب إعادة وضوئه لأن الخارج منه من قبل أو دبر أو غيرهما ليس بحدث لأن الموت حدث كالخارج فلما لم يؤثر الموت في الوضوء وهو موجود | يوثر الخارج. وضبط في معراج الدراية الغُسل هنا بالضم وفي العناية يجوز فيه الضم والفتح. وذكر في السراج الوهاج من بحث الطهارة أنه بفتح الغين كغسل الثوب قال : والضابط أنك إذا أضفت إلى المغسول فتحت وإذا أضفت إلى غير المغسول ضممت قوله: (ونشف في ثوب كيلا يبتل أكفانه وفي الولواجية : المنديل الذي يمسح به الميت بعد الغسل كالمنديل الذي يمسح به الحي ا هـ . يعني أنه طاهر قوله : (وجعل الحنوط على رأسه ولحيته) لأن التطيب سنة . وذكر الرازي أن هذا الجعل مستحب والحنوط عطر مركب من أشياء طيبة ولا بأس بسائر الطيب غير الزعفران والورس اعتباراً بالحياة وقد ورد النهي عن المزعفر للرجال وبهذا يعلم جهل من يجعل الزعفران في الكفن عند رأس الميت في زماننا قوله : (والكافور على مساجده) زيادة في تكرمتها وصيانة للميت عن سرعة الفساد وهي موضع سجوده جمع مسجد بالفتح لا غير كذا في المغرب. واختلف فيها فذكر السرخسي أنها الجبهة والأنف
نجاسة، وسيأتي عن القنية في شرح قوله وشرطها إسلام الميت وطهارته» أن طهارة الثوب والمكان والبدن شرط في حق الإمام والميت جميعاً قوله : (فغير صحيح عبر في المعراج بقوله «فبعيد». قال في النهر : وهذا أولى من قول البحر لأن الواو لا تفيد ترتيباً، غاية الأمر أنه لم يذكر كيفية الغسلات مرتبة كما أنه لم يفصل في ميائها بين القراح وغيره.
Book of Funerals. On him [pour] water boiled with lote leaves or soapwort, otherwise plain water, and wash his head and beard with marshmallow, and lay him on his left side so that water reaches what is adjacent to the board from him, then on his right side likewise, then sit him up leaning against something and gently wipe his belly, and whatever comes out of him wash it, and do not repeat his washing, and dry him with a cloth, and put perfume on his head and beard and camphor on his prostration points, and do not comb his hair or beard, and... With lote leaves: it is according to us, and it is the apparent narration like the junub, and in one narration no in both, but he does not delay washing his feet in this ablution nor begin with washing his hands, rather his face, so he differs from the junub in both, thus in al-Muhit. He did not mention istinja' due to difference of opinion: according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he performs istinja', and according to Abu Yusuf no. He made it general including adult and child, except the child who does not understand prayer, he is not given ablution because he is not such that he would pray. His statement: (And pour on him boiled water or soapwort) is an exaggeration in cleaning because heating water in such a way increases achieving the desired end, so it is legally required. What is thought to be a hindrance—that its heat causes dissolution of internal contents so much comes out—because the aim is achieved since by expelling the internal contents full cleanliness is obtained, and safety is a reason, not a hindrance, from soiling the shroud when the carriers move him. So according to us, hot water is better in all cases. Al-hurd is unground soapwort, and al-mughli is from making boil, not from boiling, because it is necessary. Thus in al-Mi'raj. His statement: (otherwise plain water) meaning if what was mentioned is not available, pour pure water on him because the aim is purification and it is achieved. His statement: (and wash his head and beard with marshmallow) because it is more effective in extracting dirt, and if not available then with soap and the like because it does its job. This is if he has hair on his head, considering his state in life. Al-khatmi, with kasr of the kha', is a plant with which the head is washed, as in al-Sihah. Al-Qadi 'Iyad in his Tanbihat transmitted the fatha only; the intended is the Iraqi marshmallow. His statement: (and lay him on his left side so that water reaches what is adjacent to the board from him, then on his right side likewise) because the sunna is to begin with the right side. What is meant by 'what is adjacent to the board from him' is the side connected to the board. Al-takht is with the kha' mu'jamah, not with the ha' al-muhmalah, because with ha' it would suggest the side, not the side connected to the board. With the kha' mu'jamah, the side connected is understood. Thus in Mi'raj al-Diraya, and by this what al-'Ayni mentioned of the permissibility of both interpretations is refuted. His statement: (then sit him up leaning against something and gently wipe his belly, and whatever comes out of him wash it, as a cleaning for him). Know that the author mentioned washing him twice: the first with 'and lay him on his left side and wash him', the second with 'then on his right side likewise', and he did not mention the third washing completing the sunna. In al-Muhit: after sitting him up, then his left side, wash him... The three? He said: (and lay him on his left side and wash him) because triplet is recommended in washing the living, so likewise in washing the dead. That he mentioned it with his statement 'and pour boiled water on him' is incorrect because it is not a washing. The three are proven by his subsequent statement 'and wash his head and beard with marshmallow' because the sunna is to begin washing them before the first washing; it is only general speech explaining the nature of the water. The conclusion is that the sunna is: when he finishes his ablution, he washes his head and beard with marshmallow without combing, then lays him on his left side and washes him—this is one; then on the right likewise—second; then sits him up and wipes his belly as mentioned, then lays him on the left and pours water on him—third. But Kawahirzad mentioned that the first is with plain water, the second with boiled water with lote leaves or soapwort, the third with water with camphor. The author of al-Hidaya did not differentiate in the waters of the washings between plain and other, which is apparent from al-Hakim's statement. In Fath al-Qadir: it is better to wash the first two with lote leaves. The author did not mention the quantity of pours. In al-Mujtaba: pour water on him at each laying three times, and if he increases beyond three it is permissible. His statement: (and do not repeat his washing) because the washing is known by the text and it has occurred once. Likewise, his ablution need not be repeated because what comes out of him from front or back or elsewhere is not hadath (invalidating impurity) because death is a hadath like that which comes out; so since death did not affect the ablution while it exists, how can that which comes out affect it? In Mi'raj al-Diraya, al-ghusl here is with dammah; in al-'Inaya both dammah and fath are permissible. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj from the section of purity, it is with fatha of the ghayn like ghusl of a garment. He said: the rule is that when you add to the washed (maf'ul) you open, and when you add to something other than the washed you double (damm). His statement: (and dry him with a cloth) so that his shrouds do not get wet. In al-Walwajiyya: the cloth with which the dead is wiped after washing is like the cloth with which the living is wiped; meaning it is pure. His statement: (and put perfume on his head and beard) because perfuming is sunna. Al-Razi mentioned that this placing is recommended. Al-hanut is a compound perfume of pleasant things; there is no harm in other perfumes except saffron and wars (a yellow dye) considering the state of life, and there is a prohibition of saffron for men. By this one knows the ignorance of those who put saffron in the shroud at the head of the deceased in our time. His statement: (and camphor on his prostration points) as an additional honor for them and to protect the dead from rapid decay; they are the places of his prostration, plural of masjid with fatha only, thus in al-Mughrib. There is difference of opinion: al-Sarakhsi mentioned they are the forehead and nose... impurity, and will come from al-Qunya in the explanation of his statement 'its condition is the Islam of the deceased and his purity' that purity of garment, place, and body is a condition for the imam and the deceased both. His statement: (it is incorrect) — in al-Mi'raj he said 'it is far-fetched'. In al-Nahr: this is better than the statement of al-Bahr because the conjunction waw does not indicate sequence; the utmost is that he did not mention the manner of the washings in order, just as he did not differentiate between plain and other in their waters.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the washing of the deceased (ghusl al-mayyit). It is a ruling statement that details the procedure and addresses internal Hanafi disagreements. The author begins by stating the recommended water: boiled with lote leaves (sadr) or soapwort (hurd), otherwise plain water (qarah). This is to ensure thorough cleaning. He then discusses washing the head and beard with marshmallow (khatmi) for better dirt removal. The deceased is washed on his left side first, then right, then sat up to wipe the belly gently, and any excrement is washed without repeating the full wash. The body is dried, perfumed with hanut on head and beard, and camphor on the prostration points (forehead, nose, etc.). Combing is avoided. The passage clarifies that the ablution (wudu) for the deceased is similar to that of a living person but with differences: the feet are washed last, and the face first. There is a difference over istinja': according to Abu Yusuf, it is not done, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is. The author explains that hot water is preferred despite concerns about internal contents, as it aids cleanliness. He discusses the number of washings: the sunna is three washings (first on left, second on right, third again on left after sitting). Kawahirzad specifies the first with plain water, second with boiled water and cleanser, third with camphorated water. The commentary also resolves a misconception about the word 'takhf' (board) being with kha (خ) not ha (ح), emphasizing the correct legal understanding. The passage firmly grounds its rulings in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) and Hanafi methodology.

Key Terms
الحرض (al-hurḍ): Unground soapwort (a plant used as a cleansing agent)
الخطمي (al-khatmī): Marshmallow plant, used to wash the head and beard of the deceased
الحنوط (al-ḥanūṭ): A compound perfume placed on the head and beard of the deceased
الكافور (al-kāfūr): Camphor applied to the prostration points to honor them and delay decay
التخت (al-takht): The board upon which the deceased is washed; note the differentiation from 'taht' (under) with ha'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on using hot water for washing the deceased?
A) It is makruh because it causes internal contents to come out.
B) It is recommended and better in all cases.
C) It is only allowed if plain water is unavailable.
D) It is prohibited because it may damage the body.

Key Takeaway
The sunnah procedure for washing the deceased includes three washings with warm water and cleansers, applying perfume and camphor, and the internal Hanafi difference on istinja' should be resolved by following the majority view.

Classification of the Deceased for Washing
Classification ruling for memorization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify deceased persons into the five categories regarding ghusl and janazah prayer, apply the rules for mixed and unidentified bodies, and explain the Hanafi exception for martyrs and rebels.

Core Idea
Muslims are washed; martyrs are honored by not washing; rebels are punished by not washing.

This lesson presents a foundational taxonomy from Kanz al-Daqa'iq that every Hanafi student must memorize. It sorts the deceased into those who must be washed, those washed without prayer, and those not washed at all, with reasons. Understanding this classification prevents errors in janazah preparation and highlights the wisdom behind the rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
The text divides the deceased into 'those who are washed' and 'those who are not washed'. Note that the first category includes two sub-types: washed with prayer and washed without prayer. This is a binary with a subdivision.
This is the primary branching point.
2
Subdivide the first category (washed)
Within those washed, distinguish those washed for janazah prayer (Muslims who die after birth) and those washed but not prayed over (stillborn fetus, non-Harbi disbeliever with Muslim guardian). Memorize these two exceptions.
The fetus is washed for respect but not prayed over; the disbeliever is washed to remove impurity.
3
Subdivide the second category (not washed)
Two types: those not washed as punishment (rebels, combatants, highway robbers) and those not washed as honor (martyrs). Contrast the underlying rationale.
Punishment vs. honor – both result in no washing but for opposite reasons.
4
Apply rules for mixed and unidentified bodies
When Muslim and disbeliever bodies are mixed, wash if Muslims are the majority. For an unknown body, wash if it bears signs of Islam or is found in Muslim territory. For a partial body, wash if more than half is present or half with the head.
These are practical presumptions to resolve doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني فالموتى ضربان : من يغسل ومن لا يغسل . والأول ضربان من يغسل ليصلى عليه ومن يغسل لا للصلاة. فالأول من مات بعد الولادة وله حكم الإسلام والثاني الجنين الميت على ما سيأتي، وكذا الكافر غير الحربي إذا مات وله ولي مسلم كما سيأتي. والثاني ضربان : من لا يغسل إهانة وعقوبة كقتلى أهل البغي والحرب وقطاع الطريق وضرب لا يغسل إكراماً وفضيلة کالشهداء ولو اختلط موتى المسلمين بموتى الكفار يغسلون إن كان المسلمون أكثر وإلا فلا. يدري أمسلم أم كافر إن كان عليه سيما المسلمين أو في بقاع ديار الإسلام يغسل وإلا فلا. ولو وجد الأكثر من الميت أو النصف مع الرأس غسل وصلى عليه وإلا فلا.
As for the second, the deceased are of two types: those who are washed and those who are not washed. The first type is of two types: those who are washed to pray over them and those who are washed not for prayer. The first is one who dies after birth and has the ruling of Islam, and the second is a dead fetus as will come. Likewise, a non-Harbi disbeliever if he dies and has a Muslim guardian as will come. The second type is of two types: those who are not washed as humiliation and punishment, like those killed in rebellion, warfare, and highway robbers, and a type who are not washed out of honor and virtue, like the martyrs. If Muslim deceased mix with disbeliever deceased, they are washed if Muslims are more numerous, otherwise not. If it is unknown whether a deceased is Muslim or disbeliever, if it has signs of Muslims or is in the lands of Islam, it is washed, otherwise not. And if the majority of the body is found, or half with the head, it is washed and prayer is performed over it, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a classification ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It divides the deceased into two main categories: those who must be washed (ghusl) and those who are not washed. The first category is further subdivided: those washed for the purpose of funeral prayer (salat al-janazah) — which applies to all Muslims who die after birth—and those washed without prayer, such as a stillborn fetus (which is washed but not prayed over) and a non-Harbi disbeliever with a Muslim relative (washed to avoid impurity but not prayed over). The second category (not washed) includes those killed in rebellion (baghi) or highway robbery (qati' al-tariq) as a punishment, and martyrs (shuhada') as an honor. If bodies of Muslims and disbelievers are mixed, the majority rule applies: if Muslims are more, all are washed; if disbelievers are more, none are washed. When identity is uncertain, signs of Islam (e.g., circumcision) or location in Muslim lands determine the ruling. If only part of a body is found, the condition for washing and prayer is that more than half is present, or half with the head. This is a straightforward ruling statement intended for memorization.

Key Terms
غسل الميت (ghusl al-mayyit): The ritual washing of the deceased body
شهيد (shahid): Martyr who dies in actual battle against disbelievers
أهل البغي (ahl al-baghy): Rebels against a just Muslim ruler

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling on washing a stillborn fetus?
A) Washed and prayed over
B) Washed but not prayed over
C) Not washed but prayed over
D) Neither washed nor prayed over

Key Takeaway
The obligation to wash the deceased is not absolute; martyrs and rebels have distinct rulings, and practical presumptions apply when identity or completeness is uncertain.

Dispute on Intention for Washing the Deceased
Discusses whether intention (niyyah) is required for washing the deceased

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that intention is not required for washing the deceased to fulfill the communal obligation, and evaluate the counter-argument.

Core Idea
Washing the deceased is a service, not pure worship; intention is needed for reward, not for the obligation to drop.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does intention (niyyah) condition the validity of washing the deceased such that the communal obligation (fard kifayah) is only fulfilled when done with intent? The author navigates between authorities like Qadi Khan, al-Fath, and al-Khaniyah, ultimately clarifying that the act itself suffices—intention is only for reward. Understanding this distinction prevents both unnecessary burdens and misconceptions about the nature of this obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the majority Hanafi view: intention is not required for the obligation to drop; the mere act of washing suffices.
This is the position of al-Khaniyah, al-Ghayah, and al-Asbijabi.
2
Identify the variant view
Recognize that some Hanafi scholars (e.g., as implied by al-Fath) argue intention is required, based on the principle that acts of worship need intention.
This view is not the relied-upon position.
3
Follow the variant's proof
Examine the apparent basis: the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' and the general rule that worship requires intention.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the rebuttal: washing the deceased is not an act of worship in itself but a service (khidmah); it is wajib li-ghayrihi. Therefore only the existence of the act is required, not its intention.
Proofs: dhimmi woman can wash her husband; angels washed Hanzalah; analogy to a child returning salam.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Note the practical consequence: if a non-Muslim or child washes the deceased, the obligation is discharged. The author favors the view that intention is not required, and this is the mufta bihi.
Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir also supports this with the ruling on children.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإشارة إلى أن قول قاضيخان أجزأهم وإطلاق عدم الاشتراط المنقول عن الغاية والأسبيجابي ربما يخالف ما ذكره تأمل . ثم رأيت الحلبي في شرح المنية بحث مع الفتح بما حاصله أن ما مر عن محمد وعن أبي يوسف يفيد أن الفرض فعل الغسل له منا حتى لو غسله لتعليم الغير كفي، وليس فيه ما يفيد اشتراط النية لإسقاط الوجوب بحيث يستحق العقاب بتركها، وقد تقرر في الأصول ما وجب لغيره من الأفعال الحسية يشترط وجوده لا إيجاده كالسعي والطهارة نعم لا ينال ثواب العبادة بدونها ونقل كلامه الباقاني وأقره عليه وأيده بما في المحيط : لو وجد الميت في الماء لا . بد من لأن الخطاب يتوجه إلى بني آدم ولم يوجد منهم فعل ا هـ . فالحاصل أنه لا بد في إسقاط الواجب من الفعل، وأما النية فشرط لتحصيل الثواب ولذا صح تغسيل الذمية زوحها كما سيأتي مع أن النية من شروطها الإسلام، فظهر أن ما استظهره في الفتح غير ظاهر بل الظاهر ما جزم به في الخانية واختاره في الغاية والأسبيجابي. ثم الظاهر أيضاً أن الشرط حصول الفعل، سواء كان من المكلف أو لا، بدليل قصة حنظلة غسيل الملائكة رضي الله تعالى عنه. وعلى هذا فالظاهر سقوط الواجب بفعل صبي يعقل أيضاً كما يسقط عن المكلفين رد السلام بفعله إذا سلم عليهم رجل وفيهم صبي فرد السلام، وكما تصح ذبيحته مع أن شرط حلها التسمية فهو أهل لفعل الواجب في الجملة، وكذا ينبغي أن يسقط الوجوب بحمله الميت ودفنه. وقال في الأشباه والنظائر في أحكام الصبيان: وأما فرض الكفاية فهل يسقط بفعله فقالوا يسقط . كذا في بعض نسخ الأشباه وفي بعضها فقالوا لا ويؤيد النسخة الأولى ما قدمناه .
This indicates that the statement of Qadikhan 'it suffices them' and the absolute negation of condition reported from al-Ghayah and al-Asbijabi perhaps contradict what he mentioned, so consider. Then I saw al-Halabi in Sharh al-Muniyah discussing with al-Fath that the gist is that what was reported from Muhammad and Abu Yusuf indicates that the obligation is the act of washing itself, even if he washed it for teaching others, it suffices. It does not indicate that intention is required to drop the obligation such that one deserves punishment for leaving it. It has been established in usul that what is obligatory for the sake of something else from sensory actions, its existence is required, not its initiation, like sa'y and purification. Yes, one does not attain the reward of worship without it. And al-Baqani transmitted his statement and approved it and supported it with what is in al-Muhit: 'If the deceased is found in water, there is no alternative...' because the address is directed to the children of Adam and no act from them occurred. The conclusion is that in dropping the obligation, the act is necessary. As for intention, it is a condition for attaining reward. Hence it is valid for a dhimmi woman to wash her husband as will come, even though intention requires Islam. Thus what al-Fath considered apparent is not apparent; rather what is apparent is what was decided in al-Khaniyah and chosen in al-Ghayah and al-Asbijabi. Further, it is apparent that the condition is the occurrence of the act, whether from a responsible person or not, as evidence from the story of Hanzalah who was washed by angels. On this basis, it is apparent that the obligation drops by the act of a discerning child too, just as the obligation of returning salam drops by his act when a man greets a group including a child who returns the salam, and just as his slaughter is valid even though the condition for its permissibility is tasmiyah, so he is capable of performing the obligatory act in general. Likewise, the obligation should drop by carrying the deceased and burying him. It is said in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir concerning rulings on children: 'As for fard kifayah, does it drop by his action?' They said yes. Thus in some copies of al-Ashbah, and in some they said no, and the first copy is supported by what we have presented.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a detailed critical refinement (hāshiyah-style) addressing the dispute over whether intention (niyyah) is required for washing the deceased to fulfill the communal obligation (fard kifāyah). The author engages with earlier authorities: Qadi Khan, al-Ghayah, al-Asbijabi, and the work al-Fath. The discussion begins by noting an apparent contradiction: while some texts say intention is not required, al-Fath seems to affirm it. Al-Halabi's research reconciles them: what is obligatory is the physical act of washing itself; intention is not required to discharge the communal duty, but it is required for reward. Supporting evidence: it is valid for a non-Muslim dhimmi woman to wash her husband; if intention were essential, her lack of Islam would invalidate it. The author then argues that even the act by a discerning child (sabi ya'qil) suffices, drawing analogies to returning salām and slaughtering. The obligation drops by the child's action, just as the angels' washing of Hanzalah (who was a martyr) shows that the act need not be from a responsible adult. The final reference to al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir notes disagreement in the text: some copies say the child's action does drop fard kifāyah, others say it does not; the author favors the first. The relied-upon position (muftā bihi) is that intention is not a condition for the obligation's discharge; it is only for reward. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on the outward act (fi'l) over inner intent for purely human-to-human obligations.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intentional purpose of the heart to perform an act for Allah
فرض كفاية (fard kifāyah): A communal obligation; if some perform it, others are exempt

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's final position, what is the role of intention (niyyah) in washing the deceased?
A) Intention is obligatory for the act to be valid
B) Intention is required only if the washer is Muslim
C) Intention is not required for the obligation to be dropped, but is needed for reward
D) Intention is required only if the deceased is a Muslim adult

Key Takeaway
Intention is not required to fulfill the fard kifayah of washing the deceased; it is only required for reward.

The Sunnah Shroud for a Woman: Five Garments and the Dispute over the Dir‘
Explaining the sunnah shroud for women and the disagreement about the number and type of garments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five sunnah garments for a woman's shroud, identify the Hanafi dispute about the dir‘ and shirt, and apply the ruling when sufficiency is needed.

Core Idea
A woman's sunnah shroud is five garments, but three suffice; the dir‘ is disputed—some say shirt instead.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explains the sunnah shroud for a woman, detailing the five recommended garments and the internal Hanafi disagreement about the term dir‘. It also covers the practical minimum when resources are limited and the sunnah of perfuming the shroud. Understanding this helps distinguish between sunnah and obligatory elements in janāzah preparations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five sunnah garments
Read the text carefully and list the five items: dir‘ (or shift), izār (waist-wrapper), khimār (head-cover), lifāfah (sheet), and khirqah (cloth to bind the breasts).
The dir‘ is sometimes replaced by a shirt (qamīṣ) due to ambiguity.
2
Note the dispute about the dir‘
Some Hanafi scholars mention a shirt instead of dir‘, and the commentator considers that preferable because the term dir‘ is unclear.
Al-Mughrib and al-Halwānī give different definitions of dir‘.
3
Recognize the minimum obligation
The text states 'kifāyah' (sufficiency) is three garments: izār, lifāfah, and khimār. These cover the ‘awrah.
The sunnah is five, but the farḍ is met with three.
4
Understand the dressing procedure
The text describes the order: first the dir‘, then the hair in two braids over the chest, then the khimār under the lifāfah, then the izār, then the lifāfah, and finally the khirqah over all.
There is a difference of opinion on the placement of the khirqah.
5
Appreciate the sunnah of perfuming
The shrouds are perfumed (tajmīr) in odd numbers at three specific times: at death, washing, and shrouding.
Perfuming is not done behind the deceased or in the grave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكفنها سنة درع وإزار وخمار ولفافة وخرقة تربط ثدياها الحديث أم عطية أن النبي أعطى اللواتي غسلن ابنته خمسة أثواب واختلف في اسمها ففي مسلم أنها زينب، وفي أبي داود أنها أم كلثوم. وذكر بعضهم القميص لها ولم يذكر الدرع وهو الأولى للاختلاف في الدرع. قال في المغرب : درع المرأة ما تلبسه فوق القميص وهو مذكر وعن الحلواني : ما جيبه إلى الصدر. وخرقة تربط بها ثدياها وكفاية إزار ولفافة وخمار وتلبس الدرع أولا ثم يجعل شعرها ضفيرتين على صدرها ثم الخمار فوقه تحت اللفافة ثم يعطف الإزار ثم اللفافة كما ذكرنا . ثم الخرقة فوق الأكفان. وفي الجوهرة: توضع الخرقة تحت اللفافة وفوق الإزار والقميص وهو الظاهر . قوله : ( وتجمر الأكفان أو لا وتراً) لأنه عليه السلام أمر بإجمار أكفان امرأته والمراد به التطيب قبل أن يدرج فيها الميت وجميع ما يجمر فيه الميت ثلاثة مواضع: عند خروج روحه لإزالة الرائحة الكريهة، وعند غسله وعند تكفينه. ولا يجمر خلفه ولا في القبر.
And her (the woman's) shroud is sunnah: a dir, a waist-wrapper, a head-cover, a sheet, and a cloth to bind her breasts. The hadith of Umm 'Atiyyah that the Prophet gave those who washed his daughter five garments. There is disagreement about her name: in Muslim she is Zaynab, in Abu Dawud she is Umm Kulthum. Some mentioned a shirt for her and did not mention a dir, and that is more appropriate due to the disagreement about the dir. Al-Mughrib said: 'A woman's dir is what she wears over the shirt, and it is masculine.' Al-Halwani said: 'Its neck opening is at the chest.' And a cloth to bind her breasts. The sufficient (shroud) is a waist-wrapper, a sheet, and a head-cover. She wears the dir first, then her hair is placed in two braids over her chest, then the head-cover over it under the sheet, then the waist-wrapper is folded, then the sheet as we mentioned. Then the cloth is over the shrouds. In al-Jawharah: 'The cloth is placed under the sheet and over the waist-wrapper and shirt, and that is apparent.' His statement: 'And the shrouds are perfumed (tujammar) in odd numbers' because the Prophet ordered perfuming of his wife's shrouds, meaning scenting before the deceased is wrapped therein. All that is perfumed for the deceased are three places: at the exit of his soul to remove foul odor, at his washing, and at his shrouding. It is not perfumed behind him nor in the grave.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage addresses the sunnah shroud for a woman. The author states that the sunnah consists of five garments: dir (a type of shift or gown), izār (waist-wrapper), khimār (head-cover), lifāfah (large sheet), and a khirqah (cloth) to bind the breasts. This is based on the hadith of Umm 'Atiyyah where the Prophet ﷺ provided five garments for his deceased daughter. There is a dispute among scholars about the identity of the daughter (Zaynab or Umm Kulthum), but the legal point is the number. Some authorities mention a qamīṣ (shirt) instead of dir', and the commentator considers that preferable because the term dirʻ is ambiguous. Al-Mughrib defines dirʻ as a garment worn over the shirt, while al-Halwānī says it has a neck opening at the chest. The sufficient minimum (kifāyah) is three garments: izār, lifāfah, and khimār, but the sunnah is five. The procedure for dressing the deceased is described: first the dirʻ, then place her hair in two braids over her chest, then the khimār over the hair under the lifāfah, then fold the izār, then the lifāfah, and finally the khirqah over all. There is also a recommendation to perfume (tajmīr) the shrouds in odd numbers, based on the Prophet's practice, and this is done at three specific times: at death, washing, and shrouding. The passage clarifies that the perfume is not applied behind the deceased or inside the grave.

Key Terms
درع (dirʻ): A garment worn by a woman, either a shift (like a chemise) or an outer garment over the shirt; here it is treated as part of the sunnah shroud.
خرقة (khirqah): A piece of cloth used to bind the deceased woman's breasts to maintain modesty.
تجمير (tajmīr): Perfuming the shroud with incense or fragrance in odd numbers (1, 3, etc.) as a sunnah practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the minimum sufficient shroud for a woman?
A) Five garments as per sunnah
B) Three garments: izār, lifāfah, and khimār
C) Only an izār and khimār
D) A single sheet

Key Takeaway
A woman's sunnah shroud consists of five garments, but three suffice; the inclusion of the dir‘ is recommended but not agreed upon.

Woman's Shroud: Description and Order
Explains the required pieces and method of shrouding a woman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the required pieces of a woman's shroud and explain the correct order of dressing according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A woman's shroud must include a shift, waist-wrapper, wrap, veil, and breast bandage to ensure complete coverage.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the woman's shroud (kafan), detailing the essential pieces and their arrangement. Understanding this ensures proper preparation of the deceased, fulfilling the communal obligation (fard kifāya) while avoiding disliked approximations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the required pieces
From the text, list the five components: khirqa (bandage), izār (waist-wrapper), lifāfa (wrap), khimār (veil), and dir‘ (shift). Note their specific functions.
2
Analyze the order of dressing
The sequence begins with the shift, then braiding the hair and placing it on the chest, followed by the veil under the wrap, then folding the waist-wrapper, and finally the wrap. Understand the logic: covering the body from head to toe.
The khirqa is placed over all shrouds or under the wrap; the text notes a difference.
3
Evaluate the width of the khirqa
Two opinions: from breast to navel or from breast to knee. The latter aims to prevent the shroud from spreading during carrying.
4
Apply the concept of kifāya
The described set is the sufficient minimum (kifāya); less than this is disliked (makrūh). The purpose is full coverage of the body.
Insufficient cloth makes a minimal covering fard kifāya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرقة تربط بها ثدياها وكفاية إزار ولفافة وخمار وتلبس الدرع أولا ثم يجعل شعرها ضفيرتين على صدرها ثم الخمار فوقه تحت اللفافة ثم يعطف الإزار ثم اللفافة. واختلف في عرض الخرقة فقيل ما بين الثدي إلى السرة، وقيل ما بين الثدي إلى الركبة كيلا ينتشر الكفن بالفخدين وقت المشي. قوله : (وكفاية إزار ولفافة وخمار) اعتباراً بلبسها حال حياتها من غير كراهة، ويكره أقل ذلك. وفي الخلاصة: كفن الكفاية لها ثلاثة أثواب قميص وإزار ولفافة فلم يذكر الخمار. وفي فتح القدير: وما في الكتاب من عد الخمار أولى لكن لم يعين في الهداية ما عدا الخمار بل قال ثوبان وخمار ففسرهما في فتح القدير بالقميص واللفافة فهو مخالف لما في المتن، والظاهر كما قدمناه عدم التعيين بل إما قميص وإزار أو إزاران لأن المقصود ستر جميع البدن وهو حاصل بالكل لكن جعلهما إزارين زيادة في ستر الرأس والعنق كما لا يخفى. قال في التبيين: وما دون الثلاثة كفن الضرورة في حقها. قوله : (تلبس الدرع أو لا ثم يجعل شعرها ضفيرتين على صدرها ثم الخمار فوقه تحت اللفافة ثم يعطف الإزار ثم اللفافة) كما ذكرنا. ثم الخرقة فوق الأكفان. وفي الجوهرة: توضع الخرقة تحت اللفافة وفوق الإزار والقميص وهو الظاهر.
And a cloth by which her breasts are tied, and sufficiency of waist-wrapper, wrap, and veil, and she wears the shift first, then her hair is made into two braids on her chest, then the veil over it under the wrap, then the waist-wrapper is folded, then the wrap. And there is disagreement on the width of the cloth: some say between the breast and navel, some say between the breast and knee to prevent the shroud from spreading by the thighs during walking. His statement: 'And sufficiency of waist-wrapper, wrap, and veil' is consideration of her attire during her life without dislike, and less than that is disliked. In al-Khulasah: the sufficient shroud for her is three garments: shirt, waist-wrapper, and wrap, so he did not mention the veil. In Fath al-Qadir: what is in the book of counting the veil is better, but al-Hidayah did not specify other than the veil; rather it said two garments and a veil, and Fath al-Qadir interpreted them as shirt and wrap, which contradicts the text. And the apparent, as we presented, is non-specification, rather either a shirt and waist-wrapper or two waist-wrappers because the purpose is covering the entire body, which is achieved by all, but making them two waist-wrappers increases covering the head and neck, as is not hidden. He said in al-Tabyin: less than three is a shroud of necessity for her. His statement: 'She wears the shift first, then her hair is made into two braids on her chest, then the veil over it under the wrap, then the waist-wrapper is folded, then the wrap' as we mentioned. Then the cloth over the shrouds. In al-Jawharah: the cloth is placed under the wrap and above the waist-wrapper and shirt, and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage details the prescribed shroud (kafan) for a woman in the Hanafi school. It is a ruling statement explaining the necessary pieces and their arrangement. The author lists five key components: a bandage (khirqa) to bind the breasts, a waist-wrapper (izār), a wrap (lifāfa), a veil (khimār), and a shift (dir‘). The order of dressing begins with the shift, then the hair is braided and placed on the chest, followed by the veil under the wrap, then the waist-wrapper is folded, and finally the wrap. There is disagreement on the width of the khirqa: one view places it from breast to navel, another from breast to knee to prevent the shroud from opening during walking. The commentary notes that the term 'sufficiency' (kifāya) means the minimum required without dislike; less than that is makrūh. Al-Khulasah mentions only three garments (shirt, waist-wrapper, wrap) omitting the veil, while Fath al-Qadir prefers including the veil, noting that al-Hidayah's phrase 'two garments and a veil' is interpreted as shirt and wrap, which contradicts the text. The preferred view is non-specification: either a shirt and waist-wrapper or two waist-wrappers, as the goal is covering the entire body. The placement of the khirqa is disputed: some say over all shrouds, others under the wrap but above the waist-wrapper and shirt, which is considered apparent.

Key Terms
خرقة (khirqa): A bandage or cloth used to bind the woman's breasts in the shroud.
كفاية (kifāya): The sufficient minimum shroud that fulfills the obligation without being deficient or extravagant.
درع (dir‘): A shift or chemise that covers the upper body, here used for the woman's shroud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To cover the head
B) To bind the breasts to prevent the shroud from spreading
C) To wrap the entire body
D) To decorate the kafan

Key Takeaway
The woman's shroud must include five pieces in a specific order to fulfill the sunnah; less is disliked.

Tajmir al-Akfan: The Sunnah of Perfuming the Shroud
States the ruling on fumigating the shrouds with incense and the number of times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fumigating the shrouds, identify the three recommended occasions, and explain the sunnah level using the Prophetic command.

Core Idea
Fumigating the shroud is sunnah, done in odd numbers up to five, on three occasions after death.

This lesson examines a recommended practice in preparing the deceased: fumigating (tajmir) the shroud. The Hanafi school classifies it as sunnah, not wajib, based on a specific command of the Prophet (peace be upon him). We will unpack the ruling, its proof, the occasions it is performed, and the limit on the number of times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The text states that the shrouds are fumigated 'first, in odd numbers'. Determine the hukm (sunnah) and what 'tajmir' means in context.
Tajmir can mean perfuming with incense or collecting the cloth pieces.
2
Locate the Dalil
The author cites the Prophet's command to fumigate his wife's shroud. Identify the type of dalil (Sunnah ahad) and its epistemological weight.
The proof is zanni in both transmission and meaning.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
How does the Prophet's command indicate sunnah rather than obligation? Apply the usul principle that a specific command (amr) to the Prophet's household indicates recommendation unless evidence raises it to wajib.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-nadb qabl al-qarinah.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf in Interpretation
The text offers two possible meanings of tajmir: perfuming or collecting. Both lead to the same practice of odd numbers. Understand this as a legitimate lexical ikhtilaf within the school.
Al-Mujtaba suggests the linguistic meaning of 'gathering'.
5
Apply the Limit from Al-Bada'i
The text adds a boundary: one should not exceed five fumigations. This is based on practical consideration and the principle of moderation.
Odd numbers up to five: 1, 3, or 5.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وتجمر الأكفان أو لا وتراً) لأنه عليه السلام أمر بإجمار أكفان امرأته والمراد به التطيب قبل أن يدرج فيها الميت وجميع ما يجمر فيه الميت ثلاثة مواضع: عند خروج روحه لإزالة الرائحة الكريهة، وعند غسله وعند تكفينه. ولا يجمر خلفه ولا في القبر. وفي المجتبى: يحتمل أن يريد بالتجمير جمعها وتراً قبل الغسل. يقال أجمر كذا إذا جمعه، ويحتمل أن يريد التطيب بعود يحرق في مجمرة. وصرح في البدائع بأنه لا يزيد في تجميرها على خمس.
His statement: 'And the shrouds are fumigated first, in odd numbers' because the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered fumigation of his wife's shroud, and the meaning is perfuming before the deceased is wrapped in them. And all the occasions when the deceased is fumigated are three places: at the time of his soul leaving to remove foul odor, at his washing, and at his shrouding. And one does not fumigate behind him nor in the grave. And in Al-Mujtaba: It is possible he intends by 'tajmir' collecting them in odd numbers before washing. It is said 'ajmara' such-and-such if he collected it. And it is possible he intends perfuming with aloe wood burned in a censer. And in Al-Bada'i, it is stated that one does not exceed five in fumigating it.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on fumigating (tajmir) the shrouds. It is a ruling with proof, citing the Prophet's command to fumigate his wife's shroud. The key ruling: the kafan is fumigated first (before being put on the deceased) and in odd numbers (witr). Tajmir here means perfuming with incense (like aloe wood) burned in a censer before the body is wrapped. There are three occasions for fumigation related to the deceased: at the moment of death (to remove foul smell), during washing, and during shrouding. However, one does not fumigate while carrying the bier or in the grave. An alternative interpretation from Al-Mujtaba suggests 'tajmir' means collecting the shrouds in odd numbers before washing, derived from the linguistic meaning of 'ajmara' (to collect). The practical effect: whether meaning perfuming or collecting, the odd number is emphasized. Al-Bada'i specifies not exceeding five fumigations. This ruling is part of the recommended acts (sunnah) for shrouding.

Key Terms
تجمير (tajmīr): Fumigating the shrouds with incense, or collecting them in odd numbers, as per the context.
وتر (witr): Odd number (e.g., 1, 3, 5) used in fumigation or collecting the shroud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
At which occasions is the deceased fumigated according to the text?
A) At death, washing, and shrouding
B) At death only
C) At washing and burial
D) At shrouding and in the grave

Key Takeaway
Fumigating the shroud in odd numbers up to five is a confirmed sunnah of the Prophet, performed at three key moments after death.

The Twelve Categories of Deceased and Their Shrouds
Lists twelve types of deceased and their shrouding rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the shrouding rule for each of the twelve categories of deceased and distinguish between categories based on age, puberty, gender, and state of the body.

Core Idea
Shrouding rules vary by age, puberty, gender, and state of the body, with exceptions for stillbirth and exhumed remains.

In Hanafi fiqh, the kafan (shroud) requirements differ based on who the deceased is. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq lists twelve distinct categories, from the adult to the stillborn, the ambiguous hermaphrodite to the exhumed body. Understanding these distinctions ensures the deceased is honoured appropriately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Locate the category in the list and note the key criteria: age, puberty, gender, or condition (e.g., stillborn, exhumed).
Categories are numbered in the text.
2
Determine the shroud pieces
Extract the number and type of shroud pieces specified for that category.
For some, the exact number is given; for others, like the muhrim, it is said to be like a non-muhrim.
3
Note any alternatives or opinions
Look for phrases like 'this is more' or 'according to us', which indicate preferred or conflicting views.
Imam Muhammad's opinion for the prepubescent girl is noted as 'this is more'.
4
Apply to a given scenario
Given a description of a deceased person, correctly classify them into one of the twelve categories and state the shrouding rule.
Use the criteria from step 1.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمكفنون اثنا عشر: الرجل والمرأة وقد تقدما، والثالث المراهق المشتهى وهو كالبالغ، والرابع المراهقة التي تشتهى وهي كالمرأة، والخامس الصبي الذي لم يراهق فيكفن في خرقتين إزار ورداء وإن كفن في واحد أجزأ، والسادس الصبية التي لم تراهق فعن محمد كفنها ثلاثة وهذا أكثر، والسابع السقط فيلف ولا يكفن كالعضو من الميت، والثامن الخنثى المشكل فيكفن كتكفين الجارية وينعش ويسجى قبره والتاسع الشهيد وسيأتي والعاشر المحرم وهو كالحلال عندنا، والحادي عشر المنبوش الطري فيكفن كالذي لم يدفن والثاني عشر المنبوش المتفسخ فيكفن في ثوب واحد اهـ.
And the shrouded ones are twelve: the man and the woman have already been mentioned; third, the adolescent boy who is desired, and he is like an adult; fourth, the adolescent girl who is desired, and she is like a woman; fifth, the boy who has not reached adolescence, so he is shrouded in two pieces: a waist-wrapper and a cloak, and if he is shrouded in one, it suffices; sixth, the girl who has not reached adolescence – from Muhammad: her shroud is three, and this is more; seventh, the stillborn is wrapped but not shrouded, like a limb of the deceased; eighth, the ambiguous hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil) is shrouded like a girl, and he is lifted and his grave is covered; ninth, the martyr will come later; tenth, the one in ihram (muhrim) – he is like a non-muhrim according to us; eleventh, the exhumed body that is fresh is shrouded like one not yet buried; and twelfth, the exhumed body that has decomposed is shrouded in one garment.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage lists twelve categories of deceased persons and their specific shrouding rules. It is a ruling statement that serves as a comprehensive reference for the shroud based on the type of deceased. The first two (man and woman) were detailed earlier. The adolescent boy (murahiq) who is considered attractive is treated like an adult man in shroud (three pieces). Similarly, the adolescent girl is like an adult woman. A prepubescent boy is shrouded in two pieces (izār and ridā’), but one piece suffices. For a prepubescent girl, Imam Muhammad said three pieces, and this is the more common view. A stillborn (siqt) is merely wrapped (yulf) and not given a full shroud, resembling a severed limb. An ambiguous hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil) is shrouded like a young girl and treated accordingly in the grave. The martyr's ruling will be discussed later. A person in ihram (muhrim) is shrouded like a non-muhrim (ordinary person) according to the Hanafi school, contrary to some views. An exhumed body (manbūsh) that is still fresh is shrouded anew like before burial; if decomposed, it is shrouded in a single piece. This enumeration highlights the fiqh of al-mukaffanūn and the flexibility in the shroud based on circumstances.

Key Terms
مراهق (murāhiq): Adolescent close to puberty, considered attractive; treated like an adult for shrouding.
سقط (siqt): Stillborn child (one who died at birth or miscarried before full development).
منبوش (manbūsh): Exhumed body; if fresh, shrouded again; if decomposed, in one garment.
خنثى مشكل (khuntha mushkil): Ambiguous hermaphrodite whose gender cannot be determined.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is a prepubescent boy (sabi) shrouded according to the text?
A) In three pieces like a man
B) In two pieces (izar and rida), and one suffices
C) In one piece only
D) In a wrap without formal shroud

Key Takeaway
The shroud requirements are detailed per category, reflecting fiqh's attention to the deceased's status and the practicalities of the body.

The Obligation of the Shroud (Kafan) and the Husband's Duty for the Wife
Determines who is responsible for providing the shroud and the exception for the wife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who is obligated to provide the shroud of a deceased and identify the Hanafi position on the wife's shroud.

Core Idea
The shroud takes priority over debts and bequests; for the wife, the husband must provide it even if she has wealth.

Providing the shroud (kafan) for the deceased is a fundamental obligation in Islamic funeral rites. The Hanafi school addresses two key questions: whose wealth covers it, and what happens when the deceased is a married woman. This lesson unpacks the general rule, the priority of the shroud over other claims, and the special case of the wife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of the shroud
Determine that the shroud is taken from the deceased's own wealth if available, and that it is obligatory (wajib).
The shroud must be from the estate before inheritance distribution.
2
Apply the priority hierarchy
Understand that the shroud takes precedence over debt, bequest, and inheritance, but cannot use encumbered property (e.g., pledged or sold before delivery).
If creditors have not collected, the shroud is taken first; if they have, it is not recovered.
3
Recognize the wife's exception
Abu Yusuf held that the husband must provide the shroud for his wife, even if she left wealth, based on qiyas with clothing during marriage.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Muhammad al-Shaybani held that the shroud should come from the public treasury if the wife has no wealth; Abu Hanifa has no recorded view.
The later fatwa literature prefers Abu Yusuf's view.
5
Conclude with the fatwa position
The relied-upon fatwa is that the husband must provide the shroud even if the wife has wealth, provided he is wealthy himself.
Sources: Fatawa Qadikhan, Al-Khulasah, Al-Zahira, Al-Mujtaba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المصنف من يجب عليه الكفن وهو من ماله إن كان له مال يقدم على الدين والوصية والإرث إلى قدر السنة ما لم يتعلق بعين ماله حق الغير كالرهن والمبيع قبل القبض، والعبد الجاني، فلو نبش عليه وسرق كفنه وقد قسم الميراث أجبر القاضي الورثة على أن يكفنوه من الميراث وإن كان عليه دين، فإن لم يكن قبض الغرماء بدأ بالكفن لأنه بقي على ملك الميت والكفن مقدم على الدين، وإن كانوا قبضوا لا يسترد منهم لأنه زال ملك الميت بخلاف الميراث لأن ملك الوارث عين ملك المورث حكماً ولهذا يرد عليه بالعيب فصار ملك المورث قائماً ببقاء خلفه. واستثنى أبو يوسف الزوجة. فإن كفنها على زوجها لكن اختلفت العبارات في تحرير مذهب أبي يوسف، ففي فتاوى قاضيخان والخلاصة والظهيرية وعلى قول أبي يوسف يجب الكفن على الزوج وإن تركت مالاً وعليه الفتوى ا هـ. وكذا في المجتبى وزاد: ولا رواية فيها عن أبي حنيفة. وفي المحيط والتجنيس والواقعات وشرح المجمع للمصنف: إذا لم يكن لها مال فكفنها على الزوج عند أبي يوسف وعليه الفتوى لأنه لو لم يجب عليه لوجب على الأجانب. وهو بيت المال وهو قد كان أولى بإيجاب الكسوة عليه حال حياتها فرجح على سائر الأجانب وقال محمد: يجب تجهيزها في بيت المال. وقيد شارح المجمع بيسار الزوج عند أبي يوسف فظاهره أنه إذا كان لها مال فكفنها في مالها اتفاقاً، والظاهر ترجيح ما في الفتاوى ما ذكره المؤلف تدبر.
And the author did not mention who is obligated to provide the shroud. It is from his wealth if he has wealth, and it takes precedence over debt, bequest, and inheritance up to the extent of the sunnah, as long as no right of another is attached to his specific property, like a pledge or an item sold before delivery, and the offending slave. If his shroud was stolen after exhumation and the inheritance had been divided, the judge compels the heirs to shroud him from the inheritance, even if there is debt. If the creditors had not taken possession, the shroud begins with it because it remains the property of the deceased, and the shroud takes precedence over debt. If they had taken possession, it is not taken back because ownership of the deceased has ceased, unlike inheritance because the heir's ownership is effectively the same as the testator's ownership, and hence it is returned due to defect, so the testator's ownership remains through the successor. Abu Yusuf excepted the wife: her shroud is upon her husband. But the expressions differ in articulating Abu Yusuf's madhhab. In Fatawa Qadikhan, Al-Khulasah, and Al-Zahira: according to Abu Yusuf's saying, the shroud is obligatory on the husband even if she left wealth, and the fatwa is upon it. Similarly in Al-Mujtaba, adding: there is no narration from Abu Hanifa on this. In Al-Muhit, Al-Tajnis, Al-Waqi'at, and Sharh al-Majma' by the author: if she has no wealth, her shroud is upon the husband according to Abu Yusuf, and the fatwa is upon it, because if it were not obligatory on him, it would be obligatory on outsiders, which is the public treasury, and he was more entitled to the obligation of clothing during her lifetime, so he is preferred over other outsiders. And Muhammad said: her preparation must be from the public treasury. The commentator of Al-Majma' restricted it to the husband being wealthy according to Abu Yusuf, so its apparent meaning is that if she has wealth, her shroud is from her wealth by agreement. And the apparent is that what is in the Fatawa is preponderant, as the author mentioned—ponder.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage deals with the obligation of providing the shroud (kafan). The author notes that the source text omitted this detail. The ruling: the shroud is taken from the deceased's estate, and it takes precedence over debt, bequest, and inheritance, up to the amount of the sunnah (customary shroud). However, if the deceased's specific property is encumbered (e.g., pledged or sold before delivery), it cannot be used. If the shroud is stolen after exhumation and the inheritance had been divided, the judge compels the heirs to provide the shroud from their shares. If creditors have not yet collected, the shroud is given priority; if they have collected, it is not taken back. For the wife, Abu Yusuf made an exception: her shroud is the husband's obligation. The fatwa is on this view, even if she left wealth, according to Fatawa Qadikhan, Al-Khulasah, and others. However, some sources (Al-Muhit, etc.) restrict it to when she has no wealth. Imam Muhammad holds it is from the public treasury. The preferred view is the one in the Fatawa that the husband must provide even if she has wealth. The commentator restricts this to a wealthy husband. The rationale is qiyas: just as the husband was obligated to clothe her during her lifetime, he is more entitled than outsiders after her death. Thus, the shroud is an obligation on the husband (on Abu Yusuf's view), and this is the operative position for fatwa.

Key Terms
كفن (kafan): The shroud; the cloth in which the deceased is wrapped for burial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa in the Hanafi school, who provides the shroud for a wife who dies leaving wealth?
A) The wife's own estate
B) The husband
C) The public treasury (Bayt al-mal)
D) The wife's heirs

Key Takeaway
The shroud is an obligatory expense from the deceased's estate, taking priority over debts; for a married woman, the husband must provide it according to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa.

Definition and Ruling of Istihlal for Newborn Funeral
Defines istihlal and states the ruling for newborn funeral prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of istihlal in Hanafi law and apply it to determine when funeral prayer becomes obligatory for a newborn.

Core Idea
Istihlal is any sign of life from a newborn — only then is funeral prayer performed.

This lesson clarifies the term 'istihlal' and its crucial role in deciding whether a newborn receives funeral prayers. Understanding the precise legal definition ensures the correct application of this communal obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term
Locate the term 'istihlal' in the Arabic text and note its linguistic definition: raising voice with crying.
The text distinguishes linguistic from legal meaning.
2
Extract the legal definition
From the passage, determine the legal meaning of istihlal: any indication of life from the newborn, such as crying or movement of a limb.
The legal definition is broader than the linguistic one.
3
Derive the ruling
Connect the definition to the ruling: if istihlal occurs, funeral prayer is performed (fard kifayah); otherwise, it is not performed.
The ruling is directly linked to the presence of life signs.
4
Understand 'tadris'
Recognize that the statement 'he falls alive' is labelled as tadris (teaching inference), not a valid linguistic definition.
This avoids confusion between pedagogical and literal meaning.
5
Apply to a case
Test yourself: If a newborn moves its hand but does not cry, is funeral prayer obligatory? Yes, because movement is a sign of life.
Movement qualifies as istihlal per the legal definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن استهل صلى عليه وإلا لا كصبي سبي مع أحد أبويه إلا أن يسلم أحدهما أو هو) استهلال الصبي في اللغة أن يرفع صوته بالبكاء عند ولادته، وقول من قال هو أن يقع حياً تدريس كذا في المغرب. وضبطه في العناية بأنه بالبناء للفاعل. وفي الشرع أن يكون منه ما يدل على حياته من رفع صوت أو حركة عضو الموت. ومن العلماء (تدريس) قال في النهاية : أي هو تعليم من حيث التفرس في أن له حياة لا أن يشهد له اللغة .
His statement: (And whoever cries at birth, prayer is performed over him, otherwise not, like a child captured with one of his parents unless one of them accepts Islam or he himself) Istihlal of the infant in language is that he raises his voice with crying at his birth. The statement of those who said it is that he falls alive is a teaching (tadris) — this is in al-Mughrib. And it was recorded in al-'Inayah as being in the active voice. In legal terminology, it is that something indicating his life should occur from him, such as raising voice or moving a limb. Death. And from the scholars, (tadris): It is said in al-Nihayah: i.e., it is teaching based on inference that he has life, not that language testifies to it.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'istihlal' (الاستهلال) which refers to the crying of a newborn at birth. In language, it means to raise one's voice with crying. Some have said it means to fall alive, but the author dismisses this as a pedagogical interpretation, not a linguistic one. Legally, istihlal occurs when the infant produces a sign of life, such as crying or moving a limb. Based on this definition, the ruling is that if a newborn cries (i.e., shows sign of life), funeral prayer is performed over him/her; otherwise, it is not. The passage also briefly mentions an analogy with a captured child with a parent, indicating that if one parent accepts Islam, the child is considered Muslim, but this is not fully expanded here. The final part clarifies that the term 'tadris' means a teaching inference that the child has life, not a linguistic testimony.

Key Terms
الاستهلال (al-istihlal): The crying or sound made by a newborn indicating life; legally, any sign of life after birth.
تدريس (tadris): A pedagogical or teaching inference, not a linguistic definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The funeral prayer is obligatory for a newborn only when signs of life (istihlal) are present, such as crying or movement.

Prayer over a Partially Born Child: Condition of Most Body Emerging Alive
Extracts rulings on prayer condition and testimony for istihlal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for performing funeral prayer over a child born partially alive and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding testimony for istihlal.

Core Idea
Funeral prayer requires most of the child’s body to emerge alive; the Sahibayn accept women’s testimony for crying, but Abu Hanifa requires male witnesses.

This lesson focuses on the core ruling for a child who emerges partially alive and then dies. The Hanafi school sets a clear condition: if most of the body comes out alive, the child is treated as a full life and deserves funeral prayer. A dispute arises over how to prove life at birth – specifically, whether the midwife’s testimony about the child’s cry (istihlal) is sufficient. This distinction has practical implications for when the rites are performed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Issue
The act is the funeral prayer (salat al-janazah) for a child who emerges partially alive and then dies. The issue is: under what circumstances is this prayer wajib?
The source treats this as a case of partial emergence with movement.
2
State Abu Hanifa’s Position
Abu Hanifa holds: if most of the child’s body (aktharuhu) emerges alive, prayer is performed; if less (aqalluhu), no prayer. This is based on the principle that life is established only when the majority of the body is born alive.
The criterion is quantitative: more than half of the body must be out.
3
State the Sahibayn’s Position on Testimony
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad accept the testimony of women (including the midwife) for istihlal (crying), arguing that men are not present at birth. Abu Hanifa requires two male witnesses or one man and two women because crying is observable by men.
The mother is excluded from inheritance testimony due to bias.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, if only two women testify to the cry, the prayer is not performed; under the Sahibayn, it is performed. This affects the application of the ruling in cases where only female witnesses are available.
The mufta bihi may lean to Abu Hanifa’s stricter requirement, but the Sahibayn’s view is also considered in practice.
5
Apply to a Scenario
Scenario: A child’s head and arms emerge, he cries, then dies before the rest comes out. Most of the body did not emerge. According to the condition, no funeral prayer. The crying alone does not suffice unless most body emerges alive.
The source also mentions that if a man cuts the child’s ear during birth and it later dies, the full diyah is owed, showing that life is treated as established when most emerges.

In al-Muhit: Abu Hanifa said: If some of the child comes out and moves then dies, if most of him came out, prayer is performed over him; if less came out, no prayer over him. etc. In al-Mubtagha: The child if his head comes out and he cries then dies before the rest comes out, he does not inherit and no prayer over him unless most of his body comes out alive; if a man slaughtered him while his head was coming out, he owes a ghurra; if he cut his ear and he came out alive then died, he owes full blood money. etc. In al-Mujtaba and al-Bada'i: They differed regarding istihlal (crying at birth). From Abu Hanifa: testimony of two men or one man and two women is not accepted regarding it, because crying and movement are observed by men. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the statement of women is accepted regarding it, except the mother, her statement is not accepted regarding inheritance by consensus because she is suspected of drawing benefit to herself. They accept women's statement because this scene is not attended by men. The statement of the midwife is accepted regarding the prayer in their opinion, and the mother is like the midwife as in al-Bada'i, but it is restricted by uprightness. They said: Because the report of a single person in religious matters is accepted if he is upright. etc. And since movement is evidence of life, they said: If a pregnant woman dies and in her belly is a child that moves, her belly is cut open and the child is taken out; nothing else suffices. And he indicated by his saying 'otherwise no' that if he does not cry out, no prayer is performed over him, and it follows that he is not washed, does not inherit, is not inherited from, and is not named. And they agreed on everything except washing and naming; they differed regarding them. The apparent position is that they are not done, and al-Tahawi reported doing them, and in al-Hidaya it is the chosen position because he is a soul in some respect. In Sharh al-Majma' of the author: If a woman gives birth to a perfectly formed stillborn, Abu Yusuf said: he is washed out of honor for the children of Adam. And the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?) said: he is wrapped in a cloth and not washed. The correct is the statement of Abu Yusuf. And if not perfectly formed, by consensus. etc. And by this the weakness of what is in Fath al-Qadir and al-Khulasa regarding the saqt (miscarriage) whose limbs are not fully formed, the chosen view that he is washed, becomes apparent. Because of the consensus you heard that he is not washed. His statement: (In al-Hidaya it is the chosen) contains heedlessness regarding the expression of al-Hidaya, for it does not address naming or its absence. Yes, in al-Tabyin: They differed regarding his washing and naming. Al-Karkhi reported from Muhammad that he is not washed and not named, and al-Tahawi reported from Abu Yusuf that he is washed and named. etc. In al-Khaniyya, al-Khulasa, al-Fayd, and al-Majmu': Regarding his naming there is a statement, said by Shaykh Isma'il. His statement: (And perhaps it was an oversight of both of them) etc. He said in al-Nahr: What is in al-Khulasa is attributed in al-Diraya to al-Mabsut and al-Muhit; then is it an oversight of al-Sarakhsi and the author of al-Muhit as well? No. In al-Zahiriyya: The saqt whose limbs are not complete, no prayer is performed over him by agreement of the narrations; they differed regarding his washing, and the chosen is that he is washed and buried wrapped in a cloth. Shaykh Isma'il attributed it to al-Nihaya and said it is stated decisively in 'Umdat al-Mufti, al-Fayd, al-Majmu', al-Khaniyya, and al-Mubtagha. Then he said: By this the weakness of what is in al-Munba' that he is not washed by consensus becomes apparent. In Sharh Ibn al-Malik and Ghurar al-Adhkar: by agreement. And what is in al-Bahr is not clear; rather the apparent is weakening of the consensus and agreement. And perhaps it was an oversight of both of them regarding the one whose creation is complete, or a scribal error. Then know that their statement here that one who is born dead does not inherit nor is inherited from is not absolute, because in the end of al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya from the muqatta'at: Whenever a fetus is born dead, he does not inherit only if he was born on his own; if he was expelled (by a blow), then he is among the heirs. Explanation: If a person strikes her belly and she miscarries a dead fetus, then this fetus is among the heirs because the Lawgiver imposed on the striker the ghurra, and the obligation of guarantee by the offense is upon the living, not the dead. So if we rule that he was alive, he has inheritance and his share is inherited from him, just as compensation for his soul, which is the ghurra, is inherited from him. etc. Similarly in the end of al-Mabsut regarding inheritance of pregnancy. In al-Mubtagha: Will the saqt whose limbs are not complete be resurrected? It is said: if the soul was blown into him, he will be resurrected, otherwise no. And it is said: if some of his creation became apparent, he will be resurrected. etc. In al-Zahiriyya: What the madhhab of our scholars necessitates is that if some of his creation became apparent, he will be resurrected, and it is the saying of al-Sha'bi and Ibn Sirin. etc.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage deals with the detailed rulings for a child born partially alive or stillborn. The author begins by stating that the ruling is to pray over the child, wash him, and that he inherits and is inherited from, and is named, even if he does not survive, out of honor for the human being. The key principle from Abu Hanifa, reported in al-Muhit, is that if most of the child's body emerges alive, then funeral prayer is performed; if less than half emerges, no prayer. The passage then discusses istihlal (crying at birth) as evidence of life. There is a dispute: Abu Hanifa requires two male witnesses or one man and two women, while the Sahibayn accept women's testimony except the mother's for inheritance due to suspected bias. The midwife's testimony is accepted regarding prayer. The text then addresses the case of a pregnant woman who dies: if the child moves, she must be cut open to save it. Next, the ruling for a child who dies after crying but before full emergence: no prayer or inheritance unless most of body emerged alive. The passage then details the consensus on the child not crying: no prayer, no wash, no inheritance, no naming, except differences on washing and naming. Some reports say wash and name (from Abu Yusuf), others do not. The preferred view in al-Hidaya is to wash but not necessarily name. The commentary then discusses the saqt (miscarriage) that is not perfectly formed: there is consensus that no prayer, but dispute over washing. The chosen view is to wash and bury in a cloth. Finally, the passage clarifies a common misconception: a child born dead due to external assault (e.g., a blow to the mother) does inherit because the Lawgiver treats him as alive for the purpose of the ghurra (compensation). The text ends with a note on resurrection: a saqt with some formed limbs will be resurrected according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الاستهلال (al-istihlal): The crying of a newborn at birth, considered evidence of life.
أكثر البدن (akthar al-badan): Most of the body; the condition for treating a partially born child as alive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what condition must be met for funeral prayer over a child who emerges partially alive then dies?
A) The child cries at birth (istihlal)
B) More than half of the body emerges alive
C) The child moves an eye or limb
D) The child is named before death

Key Takeaway
Funeral prayer for a partially born child depends on most of the body emerging alive, and the testimony for life signs is subject to intra-Hanafi dispute.

Washing and Naming a Stillborn: Dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Dispute on ghusl and tasmiyah for a perfectly formed stillborn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on washing and naming a stillborn (saqt) and identify the mufta bihi (preferred view).

Core Idea
A perfectly formed stillborn is washed out of honor for humanity; the naming is disputed but generally not required.

When a child is born dead (stillborn) with fully formed limbs, the school differs on whether the body should be washed and named. Abu Yusuf argues for washing and naming based on the human dignity (ikram), while Muhammad holds that it should simply be wrapped in a cloth. Later authorities have favored washing, but naming remains optional. This lesson explores the proofs and the practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject
The subject is a stillborn child (saqt) that is perfectly formed (tamm al-khilqah). The question: is ghusl (washing) wajib or not? Is naming (tasmiyah) required?
The passage distinguishes between stillborn and fully formed; if not fully formed, consensus on no washing.
2
State Abu Yusuf's Position
Abu Yusuf holds: the stillborn is washed out of honor for the children of Adam (ikraman li-Bani Adam). He also supports naming.
The evidence is the general honor due to a human form.
3
State Muhammad's Position
Muhammad (and in some reports Abu Hanifa) says: the stillborn is not washed but wrapped in a cloth (yudarraj fi khirqah). Naming is also not required.
The reasoning is that the child never lived, so normal funeral rites are not applicable.
4
Identify the Chosen View (Mufta Bihi)
Many later Hanafi authorities, including al-Marghinani in al-Hidaya, choose Abu Yusuf's view to wash the saqt if perfectly formed. Naming is less certain; some say it is commendable, others leave it optional.
The source highlights that al-Tahawi reports both, and the predominant practice is to wash and wrap.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practical difference: if a perfectly formed stillborn is born, one opinion says wash, the other says wrap without washing. The mufta bihi is to wash. Naming is not obligatory but may be done.
The source also notes that if the limbs are not fully formed, consensus is no prayer and no washing.

Inheritance of a Miscarried Fetus: Exception When Expelled by Assault
Exception: stillborn caused by assault inherits due to ghurra obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the exceptional ruling that a fetus miscarried dead due to external assault inherits, and identify the reasoning based on the obligation of ghurra.

Core Idea
A stillborn caused by assault inherits because the Lawgiver treats the fetus as alive for the purpose of compensation (ghurra).

A general rule states that a child born dead does not inherit nor is inherited from. However, this lesson reveals an important exception: if the death results from a blow to the mother, the fetus is considered to have been alive for inheritance purposes. This is because the Lawgiver imposed a ghurra (compensation) on the assailant, which is owed only for a living being. Therefore, the fetus is treated as alive for inheritance, and his share passes to his heirs. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The general statement: 'one who is born dead does not inherit nor is inherited from.' This applies when the child is born dead without external cause.
The source says this rule is not absolute.
2
State the Exception
Exception: if the fetus is expelled dead due to a blow (e.g., a person strikes the mother's belly), then the fetus inherits and is inherited from.
The source uses the term 'fassa' (expelled) to indicate forced miscarriage.
3
Understand the Proof
The Lawgiver imposed a ghurra (one-tenth of diyah) on the assailant for causing the miscarriage. This ghurra is compensation for taking a life, implying the fetus was considered alive at the time of the assault.
Had the fetus been dead, no compensation would be due.
4
Apply the Reasoning
Since the fetus is treated as alive for the ghurra, it follows that he is alive for inheritance too: he can inherit from the mother and his share is inherited from him (including the ghurra itself).
The source says: 'if we rule that he was alive, he has inheritance and his share is inherited from him.'
5
Note the Scope
This exception applies only to forced miscarriage, not to a natural stillbirth. The general rule remains for natural cases.
The source also mentions that the fetus is considered a 'heir' (min jumlat al-warathah) in this scenario.
