The author did not speak about transferring the deceased from one place to another before burial. It is said in al-Waqi'at and al-Tajnīs: The killed person... [interrupted] His saying: (concerning its recommendation) said in al-Nahr: It is better. His saying: (places called 'Fasaqi') it is like a house built with structure that accommodates a standing group and similar. Thus in al-Imdad. His saying: (And it) refers to the disliked. It is narrated from Aisha that she visited the grave of her brother Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Bakr, who died in Syria and was carried from there. She said: 'If I had the authority, I would not have transported you and would have buried you where you died. But if it is a mile or two or similar, there is no harm. If transported from one country to another, I see no sin in it.' Because it is narrated that Ya'qub died in Egypt and was carried to Syria, and Musa carried the coffin of Yusuf after a long time from Egypt to Syria so that his bones would be with his ancestors. Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas died in a village four farsakhs from Madinah and was carried on men's shoulders to Madinah. In al-Tabyin: If the deceased has decomposed and become dust, it is permissible to bury another in his grave, to plant, and to build upon it. In al-Waqi'at: The bones of Jews have sanctity when found in their graves like the sanctity of Muslims' bones, so they should not be broken because the dhimmi is protected from harm during his life due to the covenant, so his person must be protected from breakage after death. The author did not speak about visiting graves, and there is no harm in explaining it to complete benefit. In al-Bada'i: There is no harm in visiting graves and supplicating for the deceased if they are believers, without stepping on graves, due to his saying: 'I forbade you from visiting graves, but now visit them.' The nation from the time of the Prophet to today. In al-Mujtaba, it is explicitly stated as recommended (mandub). It is said it is prohibited for women, but the correct view is that permission is established for both. He taught the greeting to the dead: 'Peace be upon you, O inhabitants of the abode of believers and Muslims. We will, Allah willing, join you. You are our predecessors and we are followers.' His saying: (or property buried with him) al-Ramli said it is derived from it the answer to a fatwa incident: a woman buried with her daughter jewelry, goods, and common property inherited from her in the absence of the husband, that it should be exhumed for his right, and if it is lost, he is compensated. His saying: (Because it is narrated that Ya'qoob) it is not hidden that this is the law of those before us, and the conditions for it being a law for us are not met. Thus in the commentary of al-Maqdisi, and similar in the commentary of Shaykh Isma'il from al-Fath. He explained that one of the conditions for it being a law for us is that Allah or His Messenger relates it, and that is not found. The narration of Sa'd, though not denied, but that which is reported from Aisha when she transported her brother is from country to country, and Sa'd's transport is less than that, but what he argued with is from country to country, so it should be considered. He said: It is firmly stated in al-Tajiyyah that it is disliked. In al-Tajnīs: It is mentioned that if one dies in a city, it is disliked to transport him to another because it is engagement in what is not beneficial and includes delaying his burial, and that is sufficient for dislike. His saying: (And it is said) it is prohibited for women. Al-Ramli said: As for women, if they intend to visit graves for renewing grief, weeping, and wailing as is their habit, it is not permissible for them. (1) Narrated by Muslim in the Book of Funerals, Hadith 106, by Malik in Muwatta', and by Ahmad in his Musnad.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage covers two main topics: transferring a deceased person before burial and visiting graves. The author notes that the original matn did not discuss transfer. He then cites various sources. From Aisha: she indicated that transferring a body a short distance (a mile or two) is fine, but transferring from one country to another may be permissible as evidenced by the actions of prophets: Ya'qub was moved from Egypt to Syria, Musa moved Yusuf's coffin. Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas was moved from a village to Madinah. However, later commentary notes that the laws of previous prophets are not necessarily binding on Muslims unless confirmed by Islamic sources. The relied-upon position is that transferring a body to another city is disliked (makruh) because it delays burial and serves no real benefit. The second topic: visiting graves is recommended (mandub) for men based on the hadith 'I forbade you from visiting graves, but now visit them.' For women, there is a difference: some say it is prohibited, but the correct view is that permission is also established for them, provided they avoid wailing and immodest behavior. The text also mentions the sanctity of bones of dhimmis and the ruling on burying another person in a decomposed grave. Finally, it gives the wording of the greeting to the dead.

Key Terms
نقل الميت (naql al-mayyit): Transporting the deceased's body from one place to another before burial.
زيارة القبور (ziyārat al-qubūr): Visiting graves for the purpose of supplication and remembrance.
الذمي (al-dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who has a covenant of protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Mustahabb (recommended)
B) Makruh (disliked)
C) Haram (prohibited)
D) Wajib (obligatory)

Key Takeaway
Transferring a body to another city is disliked (makruh) due to delay in burial, but short distances are permissible.

Visiting Graves: Recommendation and Etiquette
Explanation of the ruling on visiting graves and related etiquette

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on visiting graves for men and women, identify the hadith evidence, and explain the conditions for women.

Core Idea
Visiting graves is recommended for men and permissible for women without wailing.

The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq again omitted the topic of visiting graves. The commentary fills this gap, citing the well-known hadith where the Prophet encouraged visiting graves. The Hanafi school classifies it as mandub (recommended) for men, and for women the correct view is that it is permissible as long as they avoid wailing and immodesty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The act is visiting graves. Originally, it was prohibited, but the Prophet abrogated that and permitted it, making it recommended.
2
Classify the ruling for men
For men, visiting graves is mandub (recommended) based on the explicit hadith and the practice of the early community.
The text from al-Bada'i says 'no harm' and al-Mujtaba says it is mandub.
3
Analyze the dispute about women
Some scholars hold that women are prohibited from visiting graves, but the correct Hanafi view is that permission is established for both men and women, as mentioned in al-Mujtaba.
The key condition for women is to avoid wailing, lamentation, and anything that leads to fitnah.
4
Identify the etiquette and greeting
The text teaches the specific salutation to be said when visiting graves: 'Peace be upon you...' etc.
The greeting is reported from the Prophet.

The author did not speak about transferring the deceased from one place to another before burial. It is said in al-Waqi'at and al-Tajnīs: The killed person... [interrupted] His saying: (concerning its recommendation) said in al-Nahr: It is better. His saying: (places called 'Fasaqi') it is like a house built with structure that accommodates a standing group and similar. Thus in al-Imdad. His saying: (And it) refers to the disliked. It is narrated from Aisha that she visited the grave of her brother Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Bakr, who died in Syria and was carried from there. She said: 'If I had the authority, I would not have transported you and would have buried you where you died. But if it is a mile or two or similar, there is no harm. If transported from one country to another, I see no sin in it.' Because it is narrated that Ya'qub died in Egypt and was carried to Syria, and Musa carried the coffin of Yusuf after a long time from Egypt to Syria so that his bones would be with his ancestors. Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas died in a village four farsakhs from Madinah and was carried on men's shoulders to Madinah. In al-Tabyin: If the deceased has decomposed and become dust, it is permissible to bury another in his grave, to plant, and to build upon it. In al-Waqi'at: The bones of Jews have sanctity when found in their graves like the sanctity of Muslims' bones, so they should not be broken because the dhimmi is protected from harm during his life due to the covenant, so his person must be protected from breakage after death. The author did not speak about visiting graves, and there is no harm in explaining it to complete benefit. In al-Bada'i: There is no harm in visiting graves and supplicating for the deceased if they are believers, without stepping on graves, due to his saying: 'I forbade you from visiting graves, but now visit them.' The nation from the time of the Prophet to today. In al-Mujtaba, it is explicitly stated as recommended (mandub). It is said it is prohibited for women, but the correct view is that permission is established for both. He taught the greeting to the dead: 'Peace be upon you, O inhabitants of the abode of believers and Muslims. We will, Allah willing, join you. You are our predecessors and we are followers.' His saying: (or property buried with him) al-Ramli said it is derived from it the answer to a fatwa incident: a woman buried with her daughter jewelry, goods, and common property inherited from her in the absence of the husband, that it should be exhumed for his right, and if it is lost, he is compensated. His saying: (Because it is narrated that Ya'qoob) it is not hidden that this is the law of those before us, and the conditions for it being a law for us are not met. Thus in the commentary of al-Maqdisi, and similar in the commentary of Shaykh Isma'il from al-Fath. He explained that one of the conditions for it being a law for us is that Allah or His Messenger relates it, and that is not found. The narration of Sa'd, though not denied, but that which is reported from Aisha when she transported her brother is from country to country, and Sa'd's transport is less than that, but what he argued with is from country to country, so it should be considered. He said: It is firmly stated in al-Tajiyyah that it is disliked. In al-Tajnīs: It is mentioned that if one dies in a city, it is disliked to transport him to another because it is engagement in what is not beneficial and includes delaying his burial, and that is sufficient for dislike. His saying: (And it is said) it is prohibited for women. Al-Ramli said: As for women, if they intend to visit graves for renewing grief, weeping, and wailing as is their habit, it is not permissible for them. (1) Narrated by Muslim in the Book of Funerals, Hadith 106, by Malik in Muwatta', and by Ahmad in his Musnad.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage covers two main topics: transferring a deceased person before burial and visiting graves. The author notes that the original matn did not discuss transfer. He then cites various sources. From Aisha: she indicated that transferring a body a short distance (a mile or two) is fine, but transferring from one country to another may be permissible as evidenced by the actions of prophets: Ya'qub was moved from Egypt to Syria, Musa moved Yusuf's coffin. Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas was moved from a village to Madinah. However, later commentary notes that the laws of previous prophets are not necessarily binding on Muslims unless confirmed by Islamic sources. The relied-upon position is that transferring a body to another city is disliked (makruh) because it delays burial and serves no real benefit. The second topic: visiting graves is recommended (mandub) for men based on the hadith 'I forbade you from visiting graves, but now visit them.' For women, there is a difference: some say it is prohibited, but the correct view is that permission is also established for them, provided they avoid wailing and immodest behavior. The text also mentions the sanctity of bones of dhimmis and the ruling on burying another person in a decomposed grave. Finally, it gives the wording of the greeting to the dead.

Key Terms
نقل الميت (naql al-mayyit): Transporting the deceased's body from one place to another before burial.
زيارة القبور (ziyārat al-qubūr): Visiting graves for the purpose of supplication and remembrance.
الذمي (al-dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who has a covenant of protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in the text, what is the ruling on women visiting graves?
A) Haram (prohibited) under all circumstances
B) Makruh (disliked) for women
C) Ja'iz (permissible) with conditions such as avoiding wailing
D) Mandub (recommended) like men

Key Takeaway
Visiting graves is recommended for men and permissible for women without wailing, based on the hadith 'Visit graves'.
